  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_dfb10901-2954-4b24-b4c2-0a02e8e4dd0f'
      });

<span id="freeTextContainer16799637543263626357">Considered the most important work of modern Iranian literature, The Blind Owl is a haunting tale of loss and spiritual degradation. Replete with potent symbolism and terrifying surrealistic imagery, Sadegh Hedayat's masterpice details a young man's despair after losing a mysterious lover. And as the author gradually drifts into frenzy and madness, the reader becomes caugh</span>
  <span id="freeText16799637543263626357" style="display:none">Considered the most important work of modern Iranian literature, The Blind Owl is a haunting tale of loss and spiritual degradation. Replete with potent symbolism and terrifying surrealistic imagery, Sadegh Hedayat's masterpice details a young man's despair after losing a mysterious lover. And as the author gradually drifts into frenzy and madness, the reader becomes caught in the sandstorm of Hedayat's bleak vision of the human condition. The Blind Owl, which has been translated into many foreign languages, has often been compared to the writing of Edgar Allan Poe.</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'm reading (and have read) the Costello translation. I've skimmed the Bashiri one, and it was too mechanical and dry. This translation is the one the<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'm reading (and have read) the Costello translation. I've skimmed the Bashiri one, and it was too mechanical and dry. This translation is the one the book's deserved reputation is based on. You won't go wrong with it. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div id="review_1105432525" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1105432525" />
    <a title="Glenn Russell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell"><img alt="Glenn Russell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1505195595p2/23385697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1105432525?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Glenn Russell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Glenn Russell" href="/user/show/23385697-glenn-russell">Glenn Russell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1105432525" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2395419369588810376"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485550600i/21826487.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>A friend once told me Sadegh Hedayat wanted the book itself to be the experience and not a book about an experience. I couldn’t agree more. So what was my <i>Blind Owl</i> experience? With every page I felt as if I was spiraling down through my subconscious and unconscious until I plunged into the collective unconscious. A female figure in a black cloak and a meeting of eyes, shinny, alluring, sensuous eyes – the anima? Another turn and there's an ancient old man with white hair and long white beard wi</span>
  <span id="freeText2395419369588810376" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1485550600i/21826487.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>A friend once told me Sadegh Hedayat wanted the book itself to be the experience and not a book about an experience. I couldn’t agree more. So what was my <i>Blind Owl</i> experience? With every page I felt as if I was spiraling down through my subconscious and unconscious until I plunged into the collective unconscious. A female figure in a black cloak and a meeting of eyes, shinny, alluring, sensuous eyes – the anima? Another turn and there's an ancient old man with white hair and long white beard with the index finger of his left hand pressed against his lips – the wisdom archetype? And yet another turn and I was walking in a fantastic landscape of trees and hills of geometrical shapes: cylinders, perfect cones, truncated cones – a dream or hallucination? And there are the eyes again and the ancient old man with his index finger pressed against his lips – a dream or hallucination or a reading of <i>The Blind Owl</i>? I put the book down and walk outside and the landscape is fantastic: all the trees and hills are cylinders, perfect cones and truncated cones and I see up ahead a female figure in a cloak. I was warned by Porochista Khakpour in his preface to <i>The Blind Owl</i>. And now you’ve been warned.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1443461682i/16362998.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="2395419369588810376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1444819640" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1444819640" />
    <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin"><img alt="Bill Kerwin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442940975p2/83582.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1444819640?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bill Kerwin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bill Kerwin" href="/user/show/83582-bill-kerwin">Bill Kerwin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9780802196422" href="/book/show/9501747-the-blind-owl">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=dark-romanticism">dark-romanticism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83582-bill-kerwin?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1444819640" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2795345767502758192"><br/>This classic Iranian novella, darkly romantic and surrealist at its core, is flecked with unsettling realistic detail and structured in a fashion which heralds postmodernism, calling into question the meaning of its own narrative, and—by implication—the function of narrative itself. It is also the source of a folk belief: people are routinely warned against reading it because doing so may cause suicide. Taken together, what an extraordinary weight for this little book to carry!<br/><br/>Its author Sadegh </span>
  <span id="freeText2795345767502758192" style="display:none"><br />This classic Iranian novella, darkly romantic and surrealist at its core, is flecked with unsettling realistic detail and structured in a fashion which heralds postmodernism, calling into question the meaning of its own narrative, and—by implication—the function of narrative itself. It is also the source of a folk belief: people are routinely warned against reading it because doing so may cause suicide. Taken together, what an extraordinary weight for this little book to carry!<br /><br />Its author Sadegh Hedayat (1903-1951), was a product of Catholic education, learning French from the Lazarist brothers in secondary school. At the age of twenty two, he traveled to Belgium for university, having promised his government to become a civil engineer, but he hated Belgian weather, hated civil engineering; soon he moved to Paris to study architecture, then dentistry, then literature. He immersed himself in Poe, Kafka, and Rilke, experimented with opium and alcohol, and fell headlong in love with a Parisian girl. When the affair ended, unhappily, he tried to drown himself in the Marne. About this time, he wrote the first draft of <i>The Blind Owl</i>. <br /><br />After four years in France, he returned to Tehran, having failed to earn a degree. He worked as a bank clerk, and hated it; he lived in a country too conservative to permit the publication of his best creative work. Although he was socially and politically engaged--forming a literary circle, dabbling in left-wing politics--he continued to seek comfort and solace in opium and alcohol. In 1936, he traveled to India, where he first published <i>The Blind Owl</i>; four years later, when Reza Shah's fall ended strict censorship, he was finally able to publish the book in his native Iran.<br /><br />It is an extremely unusual book. It tells two stories—or one story in two different forms, surreal and then realistic—involving a bitter young opium addict, a woman he loathes and loves, a cackling old man who wears a turban, and a sudden--perhaps violent--death. The surreal story, which comes first, is the more powerful, its bare-bones narrative rendered hypnotic through artful repetitions of evocative imagery, and it both enriches and undermines the realistic tale which comes after. Its narration reminds one of “The Tell-tale Heart,” its hopelessness of the parables of Kafka, but the only book I know that it actually resembles is James Hogg's 1824 novel, <i>The Private Memoirs and Confessions of a Justified Sinner, Written by Himself: With a Detail of Curious Traditionary Facts and Other Evidence by the Editor</i>--only here it is the later, more realistic story which calls the earlier narrative into question.<br /><br />It is a gripping, memorable literary experience, and I would advise you to read it. And, no, I do not think it will compel you to commit suicide. It is true, though, that Hedayat did commit suicide, but his death followed years of addiction and disillusionment. After allocating money for his shroud and burial, after stopping up the gaps in his windows and doors, he turned on the gas in his tiny apartment in the heart of the great city of Paris, where years ago his book <i>The Blind Owl</i> had begun.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2795345767502758192" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_63138485" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/63138485" />
    <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani"><img alt="Ahmad Sharabiani" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1194930432p2/614778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/63138485?book_show_action=true">Jul 12, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/4768974">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=surrealism">surrealism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=iranian">iranian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=novels">novels</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/614778-ahmad-sharabiani?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer63138485" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9019938490757559110">بوف کور = Boof-e koor = The Blind Owl, Sadegh Hedayat<br/><br/>The Blind Owl (1936) is Sadegh Hedayat's magnum opus and a major literary work of 20th century Iran. Written in Persian, it tells the story of an unnamed pen case painter, the narrator, who sees in his macabre, feverish nightmares that <i>"the presence of death annihilates all that is imaginary. We are the offspring of death and death delivers us from the tantalizing, fraudulent attractions of life; it is death that beckons us from the depths of </i></span>
  <span id="freeText9019938490757559110" style="display:none">بوف کور = Boof-e koor = The Blind Owl, Sadegh Hedayat<br /><br />The Blind Owl (1936) is Sadegh Hedayat's magnum opus and a major literary work of 20th century Iran. Written in Persian, it tells the story of an unnamed pen case painter, the narrator, who sees in his macabre, feverish nightmares that <i>"the presence of death annihilates all that is imaginary. We are the offspring of death and death delivers us from the tantalizing, fraudulent attractions of life; it is death that beckons us from the depths of life. If at times we come to a halt, we do so to hear the call of death... Throughout our lives, the finger of death points at us."</i> <br /><br />The narrator addresses his murderous confessions to the shadow on his wall resembling an owl. His confessions do not follow a linear progression of events and often repeat and layer themselves thematically, thus lending to the open-ended nature of interpretation of the story.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش اول ماه سپتامبر سال 1969میلادی؛ تاریخ خوانش این نسخه: ماه سپتامبر سال 1971میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: بوف کور؛ اثر: صادق هدایت؛ تهران، جاویدان، 1315، در 87ص؛ چاپ دیگر تهران، امیرکبیر، چاپ یازدهم 1344، در 88ص؛ چاپ سیزدهم، 1349، در 116ص؛ <br /><br />نمیدانم چندبار تجدید چاپ شده تا آنجا که جستم در سال 1383هجری خورشیدی، نشر مرکز در نسخه چاپی نوشته بود: چاپ هفتاد و پنج؛ <br /><br /><b>هشدار: اگر کسی هنوز این کتاب را نخوانده، و میخواهد «بوف کور» را بخواند، ادامه ی خوانش ریویو، داستان را لو میدهد؛</b><br /><br />کتاب «بوف کور» نخستین بار در سال 1315هجری خورشیدی (1937میلادی)، و در «بمبئی» چاپ شد؛ روانشاد «صادق هدایت»، نسخه ی دست‌نویس «بوف کور» را در حدود پنجاه نسخه، به صورت پلی‌ کپی چاپ کردند؛ <br /><br />کتاب با این جملات مشهور آغاز می‌شود: «در زندگی زخم‌هایی هست، که مثل خوره روح را آهسته و در انزوا می‌خورد، و می‌تراشد؛ این دردها را نمی‌شود به کسی اظهار کرد؛ چون عموماً عادت دارند، که این دردهای باورنکردنی را، جزء اتفاقات و پیش‌آمدهای نادر و عجیب بشمارند، و اگر کسی بگوید یا بنویسد، مردم بر سَبیل عقاید جاری، و عقاید خودشان سعی می‌کنند، آنرا با لبخند شکاک و تمسخرآمیز، تلقی بکنند.؛ زیرا بشر هنوز چاره و دوایی برایش پیدا نکرده ...». پایان نقل<br /><br />در بخش نخست؛ اول شخص - که ساکن خانه‌ ای در بیرون خندق در «شهر ری» است - به شرح یکی از دردهای خوره‌ وار می‌پردازد، که برای خودش روی داده است؛ وی که حرفه ی نقاشی روی قلمدان را اختیار کرده‌، هماره نقشی یکسان بر روی قلمدان، از دختری در لباس سیاه، میکشد، که شاخه‌ ای گل نیلوفر آبی، به پیرمردی که به حالت جوکیان هند چمباتمه زده، و زیر درخت سروی بنشسته، هدیه می‌دهد.؛ میان دختر و پیرمرد، جوی آبی وجود دارد.؛ ماجرا از آنجا آغاز می‌شود، که روزی راوی، از سوراخ رف پستوی خانه‌ اش - که گویا اصلاً چنین سوراخی وجود نداشته‌ است - منظره‌ ای را که همواره نقاشی می‌کرده‌، می‌بیند؛ و مفتون نگاه دختر (اثیری = فرشته) شده؛ زندگیش دگرگون می‌شود؛ هنگام غروب‌، دختر را نشسته در کنار در خانه‌ اش می‌یابد؛ دختر سپس در رخت‌خواب راوی، به طرز اسرارآمیزی جان می‌دهد؛ راوی چشم‌های دختر را نقاشی میکند، تا آنها را برای خود جاودانه کند؛ سپس دختر اثیری را قطعه قطعه کرده، داخل چمدانی میگذارد، و به گورستان می‌برد.؛ گورکنی که گور دختر را می‌کـَنـَد، در گور گلدانی می‌یابد، که بعدا به رسم یادگاری، به راوی داده می‌شود.؛ راوی پس از بازگشت به خانه، در کمال ناباوری درمی‌یابد؛ که بر روی گلدان، یک جفت چشم؛ درست همانند آن دو چشمی که همان شب کشیده‌ بود، نقاشی شده‌ است؛ سپس راوی تصمیم می‌گیرد، برای مرتب کردن افکارش، نقاشی خود، و نقاشی گلدان را، جلوی منقل تریاک، روبروی خود بگذارد، و تریاک بکشد.؛ راوی با تریاک به حالت خلسه می‌رود، و در عالم رؤیا، به سده‌ های پیشین برمیگردد، و خود را در محیطی تازه می‌یابد؛ که علی‌رغم جدید بودن، برایش کاملاً آشناست؛<br /><br /> بخش دوم: ماجرای راوی، در دنیای تازه (چندین سده پیش) است؛ راوی مشغول نوشتن، و شرح ماجرا، برای سایه‌ اش می‌شود؛ که شکل جغد است، جغد هرآنچه را راوی می‌نویسد، می‌بلعد؛ راوی، جوانی بیمار و رنجور است، که زنش (راوی او را به نام اصلی نمی‌خواند بلکه وی را لکاته می‌نامد) از وی تمکین نمی‌کند، یعنی به همبستری با شوهرش راضی نیست؛ ولی لکاته، ده‌ ها فاسق دارد؛ ویژگی‌های ظاهری «لکاته»، درست همانند ویژگی‌های ظاهری «دختر اثیری»، در بخش نخست رمان است؛ راوی همچنین، به ماجرای آشنایی پدرش با مادر خویش (که یک رقاصهٔ هندی بوده‌ است) اشاره می‌کند، و اینکه از کودکی، نزد عمه‌ اش (مادر «لکاته»)، بزرگ شده‌ است؛ راوی در این بخش دوم رمان، به تقابل خود، و رجّاله‌ ها نیز اشاره می‌کند، و از آنها متنفر است؛ راوی باور دارد، که دنیای بیرونی، دنیای رجاله‌ هاست؛ رجّاله‌ ها از نظر او: «هریک دهانی هستند، با مشتی روده، که دائم دنبال پول و شهوت می‌دوند».؛ پرستار راوی، دایه ی پیر هموست، که دایه ی «لکاته» نیز بوده‌ است؛ و به طرز احمقانه ی خویش (از دید راوی)، به تسکین آلام راوی می‌پردازد؛ و برایش حکیم می‌آورد، و فال گوش می‌ایستد؛ و معجون‌های گوناگون، به وی می‌خوراند.؛ روبروی خانه ی راوی، پیرمرد مرموزی (پیرمرد خِنزِرپِنزِری)، همواره بساط خویش را پهن کرده‌ است.؛ پیرمرد از نظر راوی، یکی از فاسق‌های لکاته است، و خود راوی اعتراف می‌کند، که جای دندان‌های پیرمرد را، بر گونه ی لکاته دیده‌ است.؛ راوی باور دارد، که پیرمرد با دیگران فرق دارد، و می‌توان گفت که یک نیمچه خدا محسوب می‌شود، و بساطی که جلوی او پهن شده، همچون بساط آفرینش است.؛ سرانجام راوی، تصمیم به قتل لکاته می‌گیرد.؛ در هیئتی شبیه پیرمرد خنزرپنزری، وارد اتاق لکاته می‌شود، و گِزلیک استخوانی را، که از پیرمرد خریداری کرده، در چشم لکاته فروکرده، او را می‌کشد.؛ از اتاق که بیرون می‌آید، به تصویر خود در آیینه می‌نگرد، می‌بیند که موهایش سفید، و قیافه‌ اش درست همانند پیرمرد خنزرپنزری، شده‌ است<br /><br />تاریخ بهنگام رسانی 08/06/1399هجری خورشیدی؛ ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="9019938490757559110" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3580195466" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3580195466" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3580195466?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3580195466" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer735965845443483740"><i>The Blind Owl</i> boasts the surreal aura of <i>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93101.The_Arabian_Nights' title='The Arabian Nights by Anonymous' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Arabian Nights</a>
</i> and possesses the chilling atmosphere of macabre Gothic tales.<br/>A lonely reclusive man envisions some mystical girl and he becomes enthralled…<br/><blockquote>Her air of mingled gaiety and sadness set her apart from ordinary mankind. Her beauty was extraordinary. She reminded me of a vision seen in an opium sleep. She aroused in me a heat of passion like that which is kindled by the mandrake root. It seemed to me as I gazed at her long, slender form, </blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText735965845443483740" style="display:none"><i>The Blind Owl</i> boasts the surreal aura of <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/93101.The_Arabian_Nights" title="The Arabian Nights by Anonymous" rel="nofollow noopener">The Arabian Nights</a>
</i> and possesses the chilling atmosphere of macabre Gothic tales.<br />A lonely reclusive man envisions some mystical girl and he becomes enthralled…<br /><blockquote>Her air of mingled gaiety and sadness set her apart from ordinary mankind. Her beauty was extraordinary. She reminded me of a vision seen in an opium sleep. She aroused in me a heat of passion like that which is kindled by the mandrake root. It seemed to me as I gazed at her long, slender form, with its harmonious lines of shoulder, arms, breasts, waist, buttocks and legs, that she had been torn from her husband’s embrace, that she was like the female mandrake which has been plucked from the arms of its mate.</blockquote><br />Is she an angel of light? Is she a demon of darkness?<br />He is elated and awed, and he feels a presence of death…<br /><blockquote>At that moment my thoughts were numbed. Within me I felt a new and singular form of life. My being was somehow connected with that of all the creatures that existed about me, with all the shadows that quivered around me. I was in intimate, inviolable communion with the outside world and with all created things, and a complex system of invisible conductors transmitted a restless flow of impulses between me and all the elements of nature. There was no conception, no notion which I felt to be foreign to me. I was capable of penetrating with ease the secrets of the painters of the past, the mysteries of abstruse philosophies, the ancient folly of ideas and species. At that moment I participated in the revolutions of earth and heaven, in the germination of plants and in the instinctive movements of animals. Past and future, far and near had joined together and fused in the life of my mind.</blockquote><br />Is he a demon of wisdom? Is he an angel of madness?<br />Suddenly he discovers that he is an altogether different man but death is beckoning…<br /><blockquote>Before I went to sleep I looked at myself in the mirror. My face was ravaged, lifeless and indistinct, so indistinct that I did not recognise myself. I got into bed, pulled the quilt over my head, huddled myself up and, with eyes closed, pursued the course of my thoughts. I was conscious of the strands which had been woven by a dark, gloomy, fearful and delightful destiny; I moved in the regions where life and death fuse together and perverse images come into being and ancient, extinct desires, vague, strangled desires, again come to life and cry aloud for vengeance.</blockquote><br />Everything is unhinged and shaky… Which things are imagined? Which things are real? Is insanity a cause? Is insanity an effect?</span>
  <a data-text-id="735965845443483740" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_926219241" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/926219241" />
    <a title="Gregsamsa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22119193-gregsamsa"><img alt="Gregsamsa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374800548p2/22119193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/926219241?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gregsamsa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gregsamsa" href="/user/show/22119193-gregsamsa">Gregsamsa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802144287" href="/book/show/8091326-the-blind-owl">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Nightmare lovers</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22119193-gregsamsa?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer926219241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2188041266972011311"><br/><b>Is it just me, or does this look like something to be buried in?</b><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1399004725i/9496790._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>A melancholic decorator of Persian pen cases (see above) experiences strange visions, dissolved boundaries between his art and reality, inexplicable longing, mysterious midnight visitors, gory hallucinatory chores, and horse-drawn hearse rides through gloomy star-lit mists, all embroidered with multiple threads of déjà vu and a dreamy sense of such enveloping accursedness that it would prompt Edgar Allen Poe himself to say "Whoa, du</span>
  <span id="freeText2188041266972011311" style="display:none"><br><b>Is it just me, or does this look like something to be buried in?</b><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1399004725i/9496790._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>A melancholic decorator of Persian pen cases (see above) experiences strange visions, dissolved boundaries between his art and reality, inexplicable longing, mysterious midnight visitors, gory hallucinatory chores, and horse-drawn hearse rides through gloomy star-lit mists, all embroidered with multiple threads of déjà vu and a dreamy sense of such enveloping accursedness that it would prompt Edgar Allen Poe himself to say "Whoa, dude, lighten up!" And yet this is just the beginning.<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>In the gaps between the clouds the stars gazed down at the earth like gleaming eyes emerging from a mass of coagulated blood.</b>
</blockquote><br>That simile might seem strained, but in context it resonates with earlier images, as Hedayat's technique is accumulation through repetition. This gradually loads actions, utterances, and images with ever greater portentious density, thus a strange unity survives radical shifts in the narrative, abrupt changes of setting, and disjointed chronology. One gets a distinct sense that beneath the fragmented surface there is a dark logic operating through a few enigmatic motifs and incancatory phrases that resurface over and over.<br><br><i>The Blind Owl</i> is distinctly gothic, decidedly "other" than Western, and clearly modernist, but this 3-way intersection of seemingly incompatible strands is braided as evenly as if it were entirely natural, while the atmosphere and psychology reside well within the opium-haze margins of the <i>unnatural</i>. The modernist disjunctions are no self-conscious experiments, but of the mode most suited to the troubled mind of a man desperate to tell his disturbing story:<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>...in order to explain my life to my stooping shadow, I am obliged to tell a story. Ugh! How many stories about love, copulation, marriage and death already exist, not one of which tells the truth! How sick I am of well-constructed plots and brilliant writing!</b>
</blockquote><br>But just after giving that reason--a rejection of other texts--he more firmly locates his inventions within a more shadowy subjective space:<br><br><blockquote>
  <b>The various phases of childhood and maturity are to me nothing but futile words. They mean something only to other people.... But my life has always known only one season and one state of being. It is as though it had been spent in some frigid zone and in eternal darkness while all the time within me burned a flame which consumed me as the flame consumes the candle.</b>
</blockquote><br>The above romance of <i>character</i> is only so consistent in his metaphors, however; his sense of self and surrounding circumstances benefit from no such unchanging stability, troubled as they are by dreaming or being dreamed, writing or being written. At times he seems to waver between writing to cling to life and writing to seal his coffin.<br><br>This book is not difficult to read; although loaded with enigmatic symbolism and surreal dreamlike vignettes, on the sentence level the language is quite simple, almost childlike, but it is a deceptive simplicity as with Kafka or Borges and, like those writers, Hedayat knows that the otherworldly is often best rendered in very grounded language. Rarely does the madness go purple.<br><br>I don't know if this book has been described as misogynist, but I would be surprised if it had not. The narrator's relationships with women are troubled, to put it lightly, but his character is not admirable nor, strictly speaking, sane, so this makes it difficult to extricate the author's attitude toward women from this strange man's view and the world it took in. The closest any character gets to receiving a name is "The Bitch," the narrator's wife, which sounds terrible out of context but within the story characterizes him as much as her.<br><br>Please don't let my vagueness lead you to think this novella is as foggy as this review; I'm more reluctant than usual to spoil the strange happenings which occur on nearly every page in language quite lucid. The spoilage wouldn't be the sort where a whodunnit is undone or a thriller's twists are spilled. This book is special; I feel a reader's encounter with it should be as personal as possible. I'm lucky to have come to it without remembering <i>why</i>, without knowing the opinions of others, and utterly ignorant of its worldwide reputation. It has been banned in the author's home nation of Iran, purportedly because it has been connected to suicides (including the author's), but despite this censorship <i>The Blind Owl</i> retains its status as <i>the</i> modern Persian classic.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2188041266972011311" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_930354526" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/930354526" />
    <a title="Mala" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5960716-mala"><img alt="Mala" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1435586901p2/5960716.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/930354526?book_show_action=true">May 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mala" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mala" href="/user/show/5960716-mala">Mala</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Mala by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/22119193-gregsamsa">Gregsamsa</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=disturbing">disturbing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=classic-ever-enduring-appeal">classic-ever-enduring-appeal</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=dream-fantasy-horror">dream-fantasy-horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5960716-mala?shelf=modern-fiction">modern-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer930354526" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7166083441553135815">"In Iran of the 1950's, at school, and at home, young people were advised to stay away from the works of Sadeq Hedayat, epecially his Blind Owl. A few young people had strayed from this rule and, reportedly, had committed suicide."*<br/><br/>A nightmare from beginning to end – and that's cause the cosmic drama of death & rebirth, the allegory of desire and its renunciation that Hedayat so masterfully crafts in the brief pages of a novella, are a nightmare in themselves.<br/>If you thought that Invention of Mor</span>
  <span id="freeText7166083441553135815" style="display:none">"In Iran of the 1950's, at school, and at home, young people were advised to stay away from the works of Sadeq Hedayat, epecially his Blind Owl. A few young people had strayed from this rule and, reportedly, had committed suicide."*<br /><br />A nightmare from beginning to end – and that's cause the cosmic drama of death &amp; rebirth, the allegory of desire and its renunciation that Hedayat so masterfully crafts in the brief pages of a novella, are a nightmare in themselves.<br />If you thought that Invention of Morel and Pedro Paramo are studies in paranoia; then The Blind Owl takes claustrophobia to a different level!<br />Hedayat piles horror upon horror to such an extent that one needs occasional breaks from this morbid tale in order to breathe easy.<br /><br />Not for everyone - it would require a certain sensibility – a sensibility like this:<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/926219241?book_show_action=true&amp;page=1">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>! in order to fully appreciate its complex artistry that carries variations on the same themes &amp; motifs in an endless recursive loop but each time with accretions, come further depths in meaning &amp; understanding – it's a way lot more than a surrealistic, opium dream!<br /><br />India plays a substantial part in the narrative but there are factual inaccuracies – <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ The Devdasi system has been followed in the south of India. I've never heard of it being practised in the temples of northern India, least of all, in the Shiva temples of Benaras/Varanasi! <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br />Ironically, for all its violent imagery; for its structural framework &amp; message, <i>The Blind Owl</i> draws heavily from Buddhist philosophy, especially from the <i>Buddhacarita </i> &amp; <i>The Tibetan Book of the Dead</i> – philosophy of the interconnectedness of all life forms, the endless life cycles through which the soul is condemned to pass owing to the never-ending Taṇhā/tṛṣṇā i.e., thirst/desire that is the source of all sorrow - the desire that titilates and frustrates at the same time, leading the protagonist to lust &amp; also to be disgusted by it, often in the space of a single sentence. <br /><br />Still, it's to the credit of this Iranian modernist masterpiece that it simultaneously lends itself to multiple interpretations** which are often complementary in nature – from the obvious psycho-fictional narrative borrowing heavily from Freud and Jung, to a more comprehensive <i>Hedayat's Blind Owl as a Western Novel</i> by Michael Beard, another reading focussing solely on aesthetics ( the narrator being a painter-writer), to a reading examining it in the context of various religio-philosophical traditions - &amp; through it all <i>The Blind Owl</i> still manages to retain its mystery!<br /><br />I read the edition translated by Iraj Bashiri but I couldn't find it in the database here.<br /><br />(*) The Blind Owl:A Personal Note by Iraj Bashiri<br />(**) <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.academia.edu/4289965/Searching_For_A_Key_To_Solve_The_Mystery_of_The_Blind_Owl">http://www.academia.edu/4289965/Searc...</a><br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://books.google.ae/books?id=yT98AgAAQBAJ&amp;pg=PA21&amp;lpg=PA21&amp;dq=Michael+Beard,+Hedayat&#39;s+Blind+Owl+as+a+Western+Novel&amp;source=bl&amp;ots=zycqBd_FMG&amp;sig=FqvKK3QufiwapGkpzlZPOWTGNUI&amp;hl=en&amp;sa=X&amp;ei=zjltU-usK42y7AaSsoH4CA&amp;ved=0CDUQ6AEwBQ#v=onepage&amp;q=Michael%20Beard%2C%20Hedayat&#39;s%20Blind%20Owl%20as%20a%20Western%20Novel&amp;f=false">http://books.google.ae/books?id=yT98A...</a><br /><br /><br />* * *<br />"Life, coolly and dispassionately, reveals to each person his own reflection, as if everyone carries several masks within him. Some, the thrifty, constantly use the same mask. Naturally this mask becomes dirty and wrinkled. Others save their masks for their children, and there are still others who constantly change their masks. It is only when they begin to age that they realize they have run out of masks. It is from behind that last mask that their real faces emerge."<br /><br />Poe would applaud this:<br />“ In this damp bed smelling of sweat, when my eyelids grew heavy and I was about to surrender to nonexistence and eternal night, all my lost memories and forgotten fears came to life: fear that the feathers in the pillow might turn to blades of daggers, that the button on my bed-clothes might grow as big as a millstone, that the piece of bread which falls to the floor might shatter like a piece of glass. I was apprehensive that should I fall asleep, the oil in the tallow burner might spill over and cause the whole city to go up in flames. The compulsive thought that the paws of the dog in front of the butcher shop might echo like the sound of the hoofs of a horse. Nagging fear that the rag-and-bone dealer sitting at his display might suddenly begin to laugh, a laughter that he could no longer control. I was afraid that the worm in the footpath of our pond might become a serpent, and that my quilt might become a tombstone with hinges that would slide and lock its marble teeth and bury me alive. I was afraid that I might lose my voice and no matter how much I screamed, nobody would come to my help..”</span>
  <a data-text-id="7166083441553135815" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_137216785" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/137216785" />
    <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely"><img alt="K.D. Absolutely" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1471967140p2/2156200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/137216785?book_show_action=true">Dec 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="K.D. Absolutely" class="user" itemprop="url" name="K.D. Absolutely" href="/user/show/2156200-k-d-absolutely">K.D. Absolutely</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780802144287" href="/book/show/8091326-the-blind-owl">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to K.D. by: </span>
      1001 Books You Must Read Before You Die (2006-2010)
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=501">501</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=macabre">macabre</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2156200-k-d-absolutely?shelf=1001-core">1001-core</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer137216785" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13922882118068636712">This book is dark, sad, funereal yet ethereal in its beautiful lyrical prose. Self-published in 1937 in Bombay, this "psycho-fiction" got published only in 1941 in Tehran, Iran by its author, Sadegh Hedayat (1903-1951) but it was subsequently banned. The reason: it caused many suicides in Iran after it came out. And well, if you must know, Hedayat also committed suicide 10 years after this book's Tehran publication. While in his apartment in France, Hedayat, at 48, gassed himself to his death.<br/><br/>So</span>
  <span id="freeText13922882118068636712" style="display:none">This book is dark, sad, funereal yet ethereal in its beautiful lyrical prose. Self-published in 1937 in Bombay, this "psycho-fiction" got published only in 1941 in Tehran, Iran by its author, Sadegh Hedayat (1903-1951) but it was subsequently banned. The reason: it caused many suicides in Iran after it came out. And well, if you must know, Hedayat also committed suicide 10 years after this book's Tehran publication. While in his apartment in France, Hedayat, at 48, gassed himself to his death.<br /><br />So, my list of 1001 book authors who killed himself/herself is becoming longer: Silvia Plath (drug overdose), Ernest Hemingway (gunshot), Virginia Woolf (drowning), Yukio Mishima (disembowelment), Richard Brautigan (gunshot), and now Sadegh Hedayat (gassing himself). What is it with these authors whose works made money but for unknown reasons killed themselves?<br /><br />Is there anything in their works? Why is this book <i>The Blind Owl</i> inspires people to kill themselves?<br /><br />Sarah Vowell, American author, journalist, essayist and social commentator, wrote in her 2005 book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3110.Assassination_Vacation" title="Assassination Vacation by Sarah Vowell" rel="nofollow noopener">Assassination Vacation</a> that the reason why Virginia Woolf killed herself was that nobody could understand what she wanted to say in her magnum opus <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/59716.To_the_Lighthouse" title="To the Lighthouse by Virginia Woolf" rel="nofollow noopener">To The Lighthouse</a>. I know that it was an exaggeration of sort (but really it was kinda hard to understand for an literary illiterate or untrained brain of mine) but if it were true, then that statement will be truer in the case of Hedayat's <i>The Blind Owl</i>.<br /><br />Think of a dying bedridden man who is a drug (opium) addict and he is hallucinating from absence of drug. He is confined in an isolated room and 100% deprived of any drug. The room and the man reek with loneliness. Apart from the bed and probably a toilet bowl, there is a table lamp flickering at his bedside casting an owl-like shadow on the wall. The dying man is disillusioned and remembering his wife (who he calls "the bitch"), his Hindu dancer mother <b>Bugam Dasi</b> (the only named character in the story) and a series of dark shady characters. <br /><br />In <i>The Blind Owl</i> Sadegh Hedayat is considered to have created the most famous Persian novel in Iran and the West (U.S. and Europe) and Hedayat is without argument the father of Persian modernist in fiction. Yet, in it, Hedayat's loneliness in real life (he is described to be asexual and living by himself during the time of his death), is very apparent. The prose in hauntingly powerful and the images that he creates in his readers' mind can really inspire us to venture our thoughts on what is there after our life in this earth. The pain that he has with his relationships with the people he knows (and probably loves) are so sour that can put trigger one's mind to evaluate his/her own relationships and probably do the same "with this problem and that, what else is there to live for?". While staring at the owl-like shadow he says: <i>
  <blockquote>"I write only for my shadow which is cast on the wall in front of the light. I must introduce myself to it." So he writes/talks to the shadow about his life."</blockquote>
</i>This part is the most haunting for me:<blockquote>It was dark, silent night like the night which had enveloped all my being, a night peopled with fearful shapes which grimaced at me from door and wall and curtain. At times my room became so narrow that I felt that I was lying in a coffin.... Death was murmuring his song in my ear like a stammering man who is obliged to repeat each word and who, when he has come to the end of a line, has to begin it afresh. His song penetrated my flesh like the while of a saw. He would raise his voice and suddenly fall silent.<br /></blockquote> In her intro to the book, Porochista Khakpour, ended her piece with a warning: <i>"Given the usefulness of his (Hedayat's) tactics with respect to that (his literary prowess), I'll then pass on what got me to these pages (of the book): refrain, reader, from reading this book, whatever you do. <b>You've been warned.</b>"</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="13922882118068636712" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer314742786" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12118045093636586975">Owls, particularly screech owls, which is what the Blind Owl refers to, are harbingers of death the world over: no less so in Persian folklore. Considering the morose obsession with death within the novel, following which Hedayat committed suicide, it reads like a last will and testament with hindsight.<br/><br/>In its entirety, this is one spectacular hallucogenic trip triggered by opium, tempered with brief moments of withdrawal when the nameless narrator (none of the characters within are named, btw) e</span>
  <span id="freeText12118045093636586975" style="display:none">Owls, particularly screech owls, which is what the Blind Owl refers to, are harbingers of death the world over: no less so in Persian folklore. Considering the morose obsession with death within the novel, following which Hedayat committed suicide, it reads like a last will and testament with hindsight.<br /><br />In its entirety, this is one spectacular hallucogenic trip triggered by opium, tempered with brief moments of withdrawal when the nameless narrator (none of the characters within are named, btw) experiences physical malaise (bodily decomposition, maggots, dismorphia, confusion, ...the lot). All of it predicated, I think, on serious mental health issues which generated the cycle to begin with.<br /><br />How to explain this ride? Its an exquisite, labyrinthian unfolding of mental maps which double back in a concertina of repetitions, splinter outwards with a cornocupia of symbols which, themselves, take on new imprints so that homogeneity of substance is constantly morphed into a verisimilitude of form.<br /><br />Heavily laden with metaphor, symbolism, existential angst and mental self-torture, this languid journey never becomes too abstract to follow: and opens up a dazzling kaleidoscope of the colour, emotion and visions of an addict.<br /><br />Magical realism which is truly magical. A favourite.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12118045093636586975" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_484687851" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/484687851" />
    <a title="Forrest" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/811928-forrest"><img alt="Forrest" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558621304p2/811928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/484687851?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Forrest" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Forrest" href="/user/show/811928-forrest">Forrest</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer484687851" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer915190280092092763">What started out to be a slow book found its pace and took off about a quarter of the way in. Normally this sluggish start would knock a star off my rating for the book, but the remainder was so fantastic it made up for the beginning. At first, I found the narration fairly clean and clear, somewhat akin to Calvino's prose, but with too much treacle and self-absorbed whining. Before long, however, I learned that Hedayat was merely setting a baseline that led into the narrator's more winding, abst</span>
  <span id="freeText915190280092092763" style="display:none">What started out to be a slow book found its pace and took off about a quarter of the way in. Normally this sluggish start would knock a star off my rating for the book, but the remainder was so fantastic it made up for the beginning. At first, I found the narration fairly clean and clear, somewhat akin to Calvino's prose, but with too much treacle and self-absorbed whining. Before long, however, I learned that Hedayat was merely setting a baseline that led into the narrator's more winding, abstruse voice and his even more surreal perceptions of the people around him.<br /><br />One is never quite sure if the narrator's opium dreams give him relief from his own unreality or whether it is because of his distorted perceptions and feelings about reality lead the narrator to escape into narcosis. Dreams, opiate-laden and "sober," are so interlaced with the narrator's version of reality that the work is phantasmagoric throughout.<br /><br />Hedayat captures these fever-dream meanderings and conveys their feeling to the reader by effectively using a sort of literary call-and-response that revisits events and insights with very slight variations that simultaneously move the reader along and tie the book together. Note that I did not say "move the plot along". "Plot," in <i>The Blind Owl</i> is a slippery thing. Events come and go and come again in slightly different form and with one character's visage projected onto another's to the point that there is little in the way of linear plot. If you're a stickler for clear beginning, middle, and end, this book is not for you. If, however, you want to become lost in another world, this definitely is for you.<br /><br />One example of this call-and-response is found in the narrator's journey in a horse-drawn hearse. As the driver sets off, the narrator reports:<br /><br /><i>The whip whistled through the air; the horses set off, breathing hard. The vapour could be seen through the drizzling rain, rising from their nostrils like a stream of smoke. They moved with high, smooth paces. Their thin legs, which made me think of the arms of a thief whose fingers have been cut off in accordance with the law and the stumps plunged into boiling oil, rose and fell slowly and made no sound as they touched the ground. The bells around their necks played a strange tune in the damp air.</i><br /><br />After the hearse driver has dropped off his passenger, he leaves:<br /><br /><i>With surprising nimbleness he sprang up and took his place on the driver's seat. The whip whistled through the air, the horses set off, breathing hard. The bells around their necks played a strange tune in the damp air. Gradually they disappeared into the dense mist.</i><br /><br />And again, a few pages later, he encounters the hearse driver again:<br /><br /><i>The old man sprang up with surprising nimbleness and took his place on the driver's seat . . . The whip whistled through the air; the horses set off, breathing hard. They moved with high, smooth paces. Their hoofs touched the ground gently and silently. The bells around their necks played a strange tune in the damp air. In the gaps between the clouds the stars gazed down at the earth like gleaming eyes emerging from a mass of coagulated blood. A wonderful sense of tranquility pervaded my whole being.</i><br /><br />This layered referencing continues, in many guises, throughout the book, lending it that quality of dream that leaves one confused, upon waking, as to when certain events took place and in what context.<br /><br />Hedayat also portrays a back-and-forth emotional state within the narrator himself. In one paragraph, he experiences "a kind of agreeable giddiness," while in the next, his "heart was filled with trepidation," with no change in circumstance other than that of the narrator's emotional state of mind. <br /><br />One feeling that is consistent throughout, however, is the feeling of shame experienced by the narrator, along with a paranoid reaction to laughter, as if anyone who laughs is mocking him. In fact, one gets the sense that the narrator feels mocked by life, and death, itself. <i>The Blind Owl</i> is undergirded by a strange form of existentialism that embraces fear of, and hope for, the oblivion of death. <br /><br /><i>Throughout our life death is beckoning us. Has it not happened to everyone suddenly, without reason, to be plunged into thought and to remain immersed so deeply in it as to lose consciousness of time and place and the working of his own mind? At such times one has to make an effort in order to perceive and recognise again the phenomenal world in which men live. One has been listening to the voice of death.</i><br /><br />Ten years after the serialized publication of the book, the author committed suicide. I am not privy to the author's internal struggles and am not familiar with his emotional landscape, but I can see the seed of his suicide in this work. In fact, the forward notes that many in Iran who read this work themselves committed suicide. Like so many other books, this is not for the emotionally unstable. This is not a happy book and, in fact, it might even be categorized as "horror" on par with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/48355.Brian_Evenson" rel="nofollow noopener">Brian Evenson's</a> dark literature. The seemingly unending river of nightmare sequences in <i>The Blind Owl</i> are reminiscent of scenes from a David Lynch or Brothers Quay film. Frankly, I'm surprised that they haven't tried to do a film version, as their style would be perfect for the dark ouvre of this book.<br /><br />So now you have fair warning. If you really enjoy the first 30 pages or so of the book, stop. Don't go any further. But if you are intrigued by the grim promise that this book holds, please don't start 30 pages in. Give yourself a chance to draw in your breath and hold it through the rest of this suffocating work. You're going to need it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="915190280092092763" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_875898317" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/875898317?book_show_action=true">Mar 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sue" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sue" href="/user/show/3642045-sue">Sue</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=iran">iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=read-2014">read-2014</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer875898317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9725147604012743172">Where to begin to describe this tale of love, madness, possibly hallucinogenic ramblings that circle back and forth and in and out upon themselves. oddly repeating certain phrases until you may be able to quote them without the page in front of you. This is a tale mostly of musings on death, with occasion side thoughts of murder and hatred. The author later committed suicide. <br/><br/>Some of the images presented in the book, especially the oddly geometric houses in which people can not live, seem almost</span>
  <span id="freeText9725147604012743172" style="display:none">Where to begin to describe this tale of love, madness, possibly hallucinogenic ramblings that circle back and forth and in and out upon themselves. oddly repeating certain phrases until you may be able to quote them without the page in front of you. This is a tale mostly of musings on death, with occasion side thoughts of murder and hatred. The author later committed suicide. <br /><br />Some of the images presented in the book, especially the oddly geometric houses in which people can not live, seem almost schizophrenic to me, cut off from any reality. Of course, the entire book really has its own reality but what is it? Is it in any way related to the world Hedayat knew and lived in, the world I live in, the world opium users live in, the world madmen live in. I imagine there is no real answer for this. <br /><br />I had marked several passages for possible inclusion in my review but as I write I realize that each passage probably is of another reality and therefore shows nothing ultimately.<br /><br />I was somewhat caught on what to rate this very strange book. What in fact have I just read? But I decided that 4* reflects the degree to which it has made me think and react if not actually "like" it.<br /><br />Addendum 4/20/14-While reading this book, another thought that came to mind which I forgot to include above was the possibility of this story as metaphor---for life in general, for life in Iran, for life under the Shah. Somehow this book being banned in Iran because it led to suicides seems like a rather slim excuse. I can see blasphemy or religious reasons being cited perhaps. But I don't know the times --- it was the 1940s, not the days of the Ayatollahs and fundamentalism.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9725147604012743172" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText18086862853423000310" style="display:none"><br /><i>The fact of dying is a fearful thing in itself but the consciousness that one is dead would be far worse.</i><br /><br />It doesn't really matter if Hedayat's protagonist in this story is drug-addled, woman-crazy or just plain insane; as the reader we are forced to inhabit this space alongside him, for the length of the story, come what may. We read and find ourselves asking, "Wait, didn't I just read this description, this passage, a page ago?"<br /><br />Is this what it is like to be in a diseased mind?<br /><br /><i>Is not life from beginning to end a ludicrous story, an improbable stupid yarn?   <span id="freeText9730794593910220435" style="display:none">A book that, sadly, missed the mark— told by an unnamed and highly unreliable narrator, who appears, at first, as some sort of a crossover of Kafka and Poe to me in all their surrealistic glory, but in the progress develops traits that are, frankly spoken, too much for me too swallow or even digest— Insanity, Passion, Love, perhaps, but also a strange kind of death wish— that of himself but also of others— that made me cringe. <div id="review_1068514908" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1068514908" />
    <a title="Francisco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2986010-francisco"><img alt="Francisco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442487409p2/2986010.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1068514908?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Francisco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Francisco" href="/user/show/2986010-francisco">Francisco</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1068514908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10700989910398668465">One of the most beautiful things about this book is how difficult it is to "categorize" it. Where in that mental shelf that I strive so hard to keep orderly can I place this? When I read it, it was like floating devoid of solid references, falling through literary space unable to hold on to anything I read before. The Blind Owl: this not knowing what peg to hang this book. It's scary while reading not knowing exactly what you are reading but only if you resist the urge to let go and let the book</span>
  <span id="freeText10700989910398668465" style="display:none">One of the most beautiful things about this book is how difficult it is to "categorize" it. Where in that mental shelf that I strive so hard to keep orderly can I place this? When I read it, it was like floating devoid of solid references, falling through literary space unable to hold on to anything I read before. The Blind Owl: this not knowing what peg to hang this book. It's scary while reading not knowing exactly what you are reading but only if you resist the urge to let go and let the book take you where it will take you. If you surrender and become part of this unusual vision you will see tiny glimpses of a place inside of you that also does not fit any category. Not even the category of good or bad or of real and imaginary. What the book does with its detailed, precise imagery and subtle repetition is to lead you to the place where the narrator lives, which is a mysterious place of spirit and mind and emotion. A place with no delineations or boundaries where a memory is in the future and an emotion is something you can touch. A place as mysterious as your own mind. I realize I'm not telling you very much about this book. I feel like I'm dishonoring the intention of the author by describing the book as being about this or that. I think Sadegh Hedayat wanted the book to be the experience and not a book about an experience. What more can I say?</span>
  <a data-text-id="10700989910398668465" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_417957616" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/417957616" />
    <a title="Szplug" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug"><img alt="Szplug" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395686229p2/1843184.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/417957616?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Szplug" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Szplug" href="/user/show/1843184-szplug">Szplug</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781847490698" href="/book/show/3697614-the-blind-owl">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer417957616" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4893081596860791209">Morphantasy, seeped in a maddened, weary bile for the ponderous chains of life set to a skipping needle. It's definitely an avenue of oscillated measure, rich with aromatic Middle Eastern fusions of sensual stringency exploding with surrealistic buzz-bombs crafted with twentieth-century precision. Lean and yet plush, engrossing, disturbing, but all without harming any actual human beings. Regrets abound at murdering the void in this way—anger out of place, anguish too primed for such a space; pe</span>
  <span id="freeText4893081596860791209" style="display:none">Morphantasy, seeped in a maddened, weary bile for the ponderous chains of life set to a skipping needle. It's definitely an avenue of oscillated measure, rich with aromatic Middle Eastern fusions of sensual stringency exploding with surrealistic buzz-bombs crafted with twentieth-century precision. Lean and yet plush, engrossing, disturbing, but all without harming any actual human beings. Regrets abound at murdering the void in this way—anger out of place, anguish too primed for such a space; perhaps one lacking the tunneled rigor of Western synthesis, blinded elements, earth and fire union, liquid marbled in ichorous umbrage an onrushing current, emolliating, warm, sedate, unto an encompassing perch, towering twenty-one stories above a horizon-spread metropolitan canvas peppered with shimmery light—all lazy lucent pinball motion, silent, shining, shuffling set-pieces of nocturnal theatre—slumped inside the most casual, most comfortable, most heatedly distracted, most pleasurably hydriotaphial of existential roller coasters—happy, planwise, vision-kissed; slyly rueful and meltingly aware in theory, poisedly decompressed as a hushpuppy slipper in practice. A circumcised gnosticism, desert-derived, is brought into the down of this drowner's stereophonic lament: bitter cucumber ends; index finger rituals; unsettling laughter; gerontophobial mirroring; knifed intentions; sinuous assassins and assignations; Joni Mitchell's <i>Blue</i> made rancid and sour from heartless murmurs; death alternately apprehended with the ecstasy of a perfervid lover and the prostrate dread of a petrified subject; it is <i>all</i> in the approach, my dears.<blockquote>
  <i>One for the money, two for the show,<br />Three for the lady dismembered and buried below,<br />Four might be the drunken cops, you'll never know,<br />So might as well down the wine, it's time to go.</i>
</blockquote></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer5989696918137781298">Obsession, delerium, disintegration. Repetition and narrative instability. Empty temples, tombs, secret poisons. Desire tuned towards death and vice versa. Basically, somehow, the entire template for 70s-Robbe-Grillet, and by extension, some of Dennis Cooper's <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>, spontaneously arisen from 1930s Iran. If the narrator (narrators?) has a desperately problematic relationship to the women in his life -- exalting or loathing without any human middle ground, only tending towards complet</span>
  <span id="freeText5989696918137781298" style="display:none">Obsession, delerium, disintegration. Repetition and narrative instability. Empty temples, tombs, secret poisons. Desire tuned towards death and vice versa. Basically, somehow, the entire template for 70s-Robbe-Grillet, and by extension, some of Dennis Cooper's <i>The Marbled Swarm</i>, spontaneously arisen from 1930s Iran. If the narrator (narrators?) has a desperately problematic relationship to the women in his life -- exalting or loathing without any human middle ground, only tending towards complete obliteration -- it only heightens the startling madness yawning up out of here. Stunningly constructed, bleakly philosophic, vividly expressionist, blackly exhuberant.<br /><br /><blockquote>There were times when I wished I could be endowed after death with large hands and long sensitive fingers: I would carefully collect together all the atoms of my body and hold them tightly in my hands to prevent them, my property, from passing into the bodies of rabble-men.</blockquote> </span>

<div id="review_570587181" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570587181" />
    <a title="Orsolya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya"><img alt="Orsolya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603139114p2/5647191.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570587181?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Orsolya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Orsolya" href="/user/show/5647191-orsolya">Orsolya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=library-2">library-2</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=other">other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5647191-orsolya?shelf=folk-tales-short-stories">folk-tales-short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570587181" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11433200027982195135">Due to my obsession with owls and having previously dated a Persian man; I am familiar with the connotation of owls in Iranian culture. Hearing that Sadegh Hedayat, the author of “The Blind Owl”, lived in Iran and then committed suicide at age 48, was enough to draw me in. <br/><br/>“The Blind Owl” has a magnetic draw starting with the first word. Following a stream of consciousness style (don’t expect dialogue, character names, etc); Hedayat pulls the reader in full-force with heavy literary, symbolical,</span>
  <span id="freeText11433200027982195135" style="display:none">Due to my obsession with owls and having previously dated a Persian man; I am familiar with the connotation of owls in Iranian culture. Hearing that Sadegh Hedayat, the author of “The Blind Owl”, lived in Iran and then committed suicide at age 48, was enough to draw me in. <br /><br />“The Blind Owl” has a magnetic draw starting with the first word. Following a stream of consciousness style (don’t expect dialogue, character names, etc); Hedayat pulls the reader in full-force with heavy literary, symbolical, and metaphorical means. This is clearly not a book for everyone; but for those readers who enjoy depth, “The Blind Owl” is a satisfying novel. <br /><br />Oftentimes, one cannot distinguish if the narrator is drugged, in a fantasy, reality, dreaming, etc; but it doesn’t matter. The actual events described are of less importance than that of the philosophical and psychological messaged conveyed. In fact, Hedayat smoothly and seamlessly digs at dark inner thoughts readers may have experienced in secret and “The Blind Owl” thus feels like reading the author’s diary (or even the reader’s own); versus a fictional narrator’s story. Although dark, the novel is a beautiful and relatable in its desolation. Even the language translation holds no bounds and doesn’t create any loss.<br /><br />“The Blind Owl” is very “readable” with a steady pace, despite it not conforming to a traditional novelistic style. Furthermore, some reviewers complain of repetitive phrases which is not an author error in this case, but the opium and maniac-oppressive/obsessive thoughts (plus symbolism) of the narrator. Again, “The Blind Owl” is not for everyone as apparently it is too heavy for some readers. <br /><br />Speaking of ‘heavy’, Hedayat doesn’t implore regular chapter breaks which along with the text, can be too much to “take in” all at once despite how wonderful the novel is. Reading breaks are encouraged for full enjoyment.<br /><br />The ebb and flow of the narrator’s consciousness and stupor mimics the reality of life and mystic thoughts and continues to release believability. In fact, some of the quotes are so clear, that they directly flow as the reader’s own thoughts. <br /><br />“The Blind Owl” briefly visits religious topics, albeit in an unexpected way of that of traditional Islamic-induced cultural studies. This is refreshing and strengthens the originality of the novel. Clearly, the novel also discusses death. Hedayat’s narrator visits this so rationally, that one forgets that it may possibly be irrational to have these thoughts in the first place. Basically, “The Blind Owl” lingers on dark thoughts with morbid appeal and yet weaves them with beauty and understanding. <br /><br />The conclusion of “The Blind Owl” is somewhat abrupt and doesn’t necessarily tie all the loose strings. However, it makes sense, feels natural, and fits spotlessly within the narrator’s characterization. <br /><br />“The Blind Owl” is an extraordinary literary stream of consciousness work. Passionate, multi-leveled, and philosophical; it <u>has</u> to be more of an autobiographical diary than a fictional novel. It if it is pure fiction, than it is a masterpiece and the author is a genius. “The Blind Owl” is highly recommended for intelligent readers. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11433200027982195135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
